Player_Black (to Proxy):

An analysis of the current position reveals that White's King has wandered aggressively to `e3`, where it is heavily restricted by its own pawns and perfectly positioned for a fatal blow. 

Let's look at the move **Qd8-d4**:
1. You can safely play the Queen to `d4`, directly checking the White King on `e3`.
2. Can White capture the Queen? The White King is the only piece attacking `d4`, but capturing is illegal because the Queen is protected by your pawn on `e5`. No other White piece can reach `d4` (the White Queen on `d1` is blocked by its own `d3` pawn).
3. Can the White King escape?
   - `d3`, `e2`, and `f3` are blocked by White's own pawns.
   - `e4` and `f4` are covered by your Queen on the 4th rank.
   - `f2` is covered by your Queen along the a7-g1 diagonal.
   - `d2` is perfectly controlled by your pawn that pushed all the way down to `c3`.

The King has no safe squares to move to, no piece can block the check because the Queen is on an adjacent diagonal square, and the Queen cannot be captured. 

This move delivers an immediate Checkmate!

make_move d8d4

A powerful tactical opportunity is available here. By centralizing the knight with tempo on the White Queen, you can launch a devastating attack.

Let's play **c6d4**. 

This move directly attacks White's Queen on b3 and sets up a lethal threat. From d4, the knight eyes the c2 square for a royal fork. Notice how deeply ingrained your d3 pawn is—it actually defends the c2 square! This means that if White's King tries to capture the knight on c2, it would be illegal due to the pawn's protection. 

If White tries to save the Queen by grabbing your b7 pawn (`Qxb7`), you can simply reply with `Rb8`. The Queen will be forced to retreat, and `Nc2+` will follow, winning a full rook and completely collapsing White's position.

**make_move c6d4**

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the current board position, you are up a pawn due to the advanced pawn on **d4**, which is a significant thorn in White's position. However, right now, that **d4** pawn is completely undefended and is being directly attacked by White's Queen on **d3**.

We need to defend this extra pawn while continuing to develop. 
There are a couple of ways to defend it, but we have to be careful:
- Playing the pawn move `c7c5` seems natural, but it critically leaves the a4-e8 diagonal open. White could respond with `Qb5+` and cleanly win your newly placed pawn on c5 (`1... c5 2. Qb5+ Nc6 3. Qxc5`). 
- Playing the Queen out early (`Qf6` or `Qd6`) is awkward and takes away ideal squares from your other pieces.

The best and most principled move is to develop your Queenside Knight to **c6**. 
`Nc6` perfectly defends the d4 pawn, brings a minor piece to an active central square, and permanently blocks any future `Qb5+` or `Qa4+` ideas from White's Queen.

make_move b8c6

Player_Black (to Proxy):

The current position reveals that White has a misplaced Knight on h3, which is only defended by the g2 pawn. The immediate capture of this Knight is tactically sound and strategically beneficial.

By playing `Bxh3` (c8h3), you force a very favorable exchange. If White recaptures with `gxh3`, their kingside pawn structure is completely shattered (creating doubled h-pawns and an isolated f-pawn), and their King—which is still stuck in the center—will become severely exposed to future attacks (such as a subsequent `Qh4+`). Alternatively, if White ignores the capture and decides to grab your undefended pawn on e3 with `Qxe3+`, you can comfortably block the check while developing a piece (e.g., `Be7`), after which White still has to deal with the hanging piece on h3. 

None of the alternatives offer such a direct advantage. 

make_move c8h3

Player_Black (to Proxy):

A careful look at the board shows that your Knight on d3 is currently under attack by White's pawns on c2 and e2. It's time to move it to a safe and highly active square! 

By jumping to f2, your Knight attacks the White King on d1, delivering a powerful check. No White piece can capture the Knight on f2, and because it's a Knight check, White cannot block it. White's King is also severely restricted: squares like c1 and e1 are covered by your pawn on d2, and moving forward is blocked by White's own pawns. 

This will force the White King to step up and capture the d2 pawn (`Kxd2`), drawing it straight out into the open where your Bishops and Queen can launch a devastating attack (for example, with an incoming `...Bb4+`). 

make_move d3f2

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To find the winning sequence, let's carefully evaluate the position of the White King at a2. It is severely restricted by your minor pieces. We can initiate a forced mating sequence starting with a Queen sacrifice/capture.

1. **d1c2+** 
You capture the white pawn on c2 with your Queen, delivering a check along the 2nd rank. White's King cannot step onto the 1st rank (a1 is covered by your Knight on b3, b1 is covered by your Queen). It cannot step onto b2 (covered by the Queen), and it cannot capture your Knight on b3 because it is protected by the Queen on c2. White's only legal response is to step up:
*(Forced)* **Ka3**

2. **c2b2+** 
Now, you follow the King, moving the Queen to b2. This gives another check. The Queen is perfectly protected by your Bishop on c3, so the King cannot capture it. The b4 square is also covered by your c3 Bishop. Once again, White is forced to step further up the board:
*(Forced)* **Ka4**

3. **b3c5#**
You deliver the final blow with your Knight! Moving it to c5 gives a beautiful check. Let's look at White's options:
- **a5** is covered by your Bishop on c3.
- **a3** and **b3** are covered by your Queen on b2.
- **b4** is covered by your Bishop on c3.
- **b5** is occupied by White's own pawn. 

With no escape squares and no way to capture the Knight, this results in a spectacular checkmate. 

Let's begin the sequence!

make_move d1c2

The move `Ng4+` is a direct punishment for this bizarre King placement. Let's look at why it works so well:
1. **It is a safe check:** Our Knight jumps to `g4`, attacking the exposed King on `f2`. The `g4` square is only under the view of the White Queen on `d1`, but our Bishop on `c8` perfectly defends `g4`. If White makes the terrible mistake of playing `Qxg4`, we simply recapture `Bxg4` and win the Queen for a minor piece. 
2. **It restricts White's options:** White cannot block the check, leaving moving the King as their only option. White will be forced to play something like `Kg1`, completely burying the Rook and losing crucial developmental tempo, or `Kg3/Kf3`, which draws the King even further out into the center of the board where it can be continuously attacked.
3. **It's a fleeting opportunity:** If we play a developing move like `c4` first, White could respond with `Be2` or `Nf3`, adding extra defenders to the `g4` square and taking away this powerful check. We must strike now!

thought
The position shows a typical King's Gambit or similar opening gone horribly wrong for White, as they have pushed their f and g pawns to f3 and g4, severely weakening the kingside and the e1-h4 diagonal. Black's knight on f6 is currently blocking the black Queen from accessing h4.

By playing the clearance sacrifice `Nxg4` (`f6g4`), Black completely opens the d8-h4 diagonal for the Queen while simultaneously capturing a pawn. 

If White accepts the sacrificed knight with `fxg4`, Black unleashes `Qh4#`. The check is along the h4-e1 diagonal. White's f2 square will be covered by the Queen itself, the g3 square is empty, and White has no pieces capable of blocking the check or capturing the Queen. The White king on e1 is entombed by its own pieces (Queen on d1, Bishop on f1). 

Even if White declines the knight and attempts to create an escape square (for instance, by playing `e3` or `e4` to run with `Ke2`), Black will follow up with `Qh4+` anyway, maintaining a crushing and overwhelming attack while being up a pawn. The knight on g4 also covers crucial squares such as f2 and e3, restricting White's defensive options even further.

This tactical sequence is an immediate and decisive advantage for Black.
Action: make_move f6g4.

The current position reveals a critical mistake by White. By playing `f3` to attack your Knight on `g4`, White has weakened the `f2` square and overlooked a devastating tactical sequence.

Your Knight on `g4` is currently under attack by the `f3` pawn, but you have the perfect counter-attacking square: **f2**. 

Moving the Knight to `f2` executes a crushing "family fork," simultaneously attacking White's Queen on `d1` and the Rook on `h1`. Normally, the King would just capture a piece landing on `f2`, but White's King is stranded on `e2` and the `f2` square is powerfully defended by your Queen centralized on `d4` (via the d4-e3-f2 diagonal). 

Because no other White piece can capture on `f2`, the King cannot legally take the Knight. White is forced to move their Queen to safety (the only safe square being `e1`), allowing you to cleanly capture the Rook on `h1` on the next turn, winning a full exchange and completely tearing apart White's position.

An analysis of the current position shows that White has launched a double attack on your pieces. White's Bishop on b2 is directly attacking your Queen on d4 along the dark-square diagonal. At the same time, White's King on e2 is attacking your Knight on d1. 

If you just move your Queen to safety, White will simply capture your Knight with `Kxd1`. Alternatively, if you capture the Bishop with your Queen (`d4xb2`), you give up your Knight after `Kxd1`.

This move perfectly neutralizes White's threats while increasing your material advantage.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

thought
The current board shows Black is up a full Queen (White has no Queen). Black has a Queen centralized on d4. The most critical part of the position is the pawn tension in the center: Black's pawns on d5 and e5 are facing White's pawns on e4 and f4. White's e4 pawn is currently attacking Black's d5 pawn, and White's f4 pawn is attacking Black's e5 pawn. 

If Black ignores this tension, White could play `e4xd5`, capturing a pawn and simultaneously attacking Black's knight on c6 with the newly placed d5 pawn, supported by the g2 Bishop. This would be annoying and give White unnecessary counterplay.

Black has two pawn captures available: `e5xf4` and `d5xe4`.
1. If `e5xf4`, White responds with `e4xd5`. Black loses the d5 pawn, and the c6 knight is immediately under attack by the d5 pawn, forcing it to move and leaving White with a strong passed pawn.
2. If `d5xe4`, Black resolves the threat to the d5 pawn by capturing the very pawn that was attacking it. The e4 pawn is removed, and Black's pawn lands safely on e4, where it is defended horizontally by the d4 Queen and effectively blunts White's light-squared bishop on g2.

Thus, `d5e4` is the most logical, safest, and strongest move. It wins a pawn, simplifies the center, neutralizes White's pawn breaks, and avoids any tempo loss.

Let's make the move.
⸫make_move d5e4

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An excellent position! Let's analyze the checks available to Black and see if we can force a mate. 

White's King is dangerously exposed on `e3`. You have your Queen on `c2`, Bishop on `c3`, Knight on `d3`, and a Pawn on `e4` all swarming the King. Let's look at the direct check:

**1... Qd2+ (c2d2)**
This move attacks the White King diagonally. Let's observe White's options:
- The King cannot capture the Queen because it is perfectly defended by your `c3` Bishop.
- Can any White pieces capture the Queen on `d2`? No. The `e1` Rook, `g2` Bishop, and `h3` Knight are all looking in different directions.
- The King cannot move to `e2`, `f2`, `d3`, or `d4` because your Queen controls those squares. 
- The King cannot move to `f3` because your `e4` pawn is guarding it.
- Moving to `f4` is impossible as it's occupied by White's own pawn.
- Thus, White is completely forced to capture your unprotected pawn with **2. Kxe4**.

Now that White's King has been baited to `e4`, you have a devastating follow-up:
**2... Nc5# (d3c5)**
This unleashes a beautiful, inescapable checkmate! 
- The King is in check from the Knight.
- The King cannot return to `d3`, `e3`, `d4`, or `d5` because the `d2` Queen dominates the newly opened d-file and adjacent squares.
- `e5` is occupied by your pawn, which is defended by your other Knight on `c6`.
- `f5` and `f3` are sniped by your dark-squared Bishop on `g4`.
- `f4` is still blocked by White's own pawn.
- No White piece can capture your checking Knight on `c5`.

It's a forced mate in 2!

make_move c2d2

This is a beautiful position where Black can deliver a forced checkmate in 1 move by utilizing a discovered attack! 

Let's evaluate the position:
The White King is aggressively placed on `e4`, surrounded by a swarm of Black pieces. We need to find a way to trap it completely.

By moving your Knight from `d3` to `c5` (`d3c5+`), you hit the White King with a direct Knight check. Because it's a Knight check, White cannot block it and no White piece is in position to capture the Knight on `c5`. 

Now let's look at the King's escape squares around `e4`:
1. **e5** is occupied by your own Black Pawn, which is solidly protected by your `c6` Knight.
2. **d5** is now heavily controlled by your `d2` Queen. By moving the `d3` Knight, you've completely opened the d-file, giving your Queen an unobstructed view of the `d5` escape square. 
3. **d4** is guarded by your `c6` Knight, `c3` Bishop, and `e5` Pawn.
4. **d3** (where the Knight just left) is immediately covered by the moving `c5` Knight itself, as well as the newly opened `d2` Queen.
5. **e3** is locked down by your `d2` Queen.
6. **f3** is sliced off by your `g4` Bishop.
7. **f4** is occupied by White's own pawn.
8. **f5** is also covered by your dark-squared `g4` Bishop.

The White King has absolutely zero legal squares to step onto and the checking Knight cannot be removed. It's a gorgeous and inescapable web!

Player_Black (to Proxy):

An analysis of the current position shows that Black has two hanging pieces: the bishop on g4 is attacked by White's pawn on h3, and the knight on d2 is hanging, as it is completely undefended and attacked by White's king on e1. 

If we look at the available moves for the d2 knight, it can capture White's knight on c4 (`d2c4`). Moving the knight from d2 saves it from being captured by the king, while simultaneously grabbing an active White piece. 

Let's evaluate what happens after `d2c4`:
- Black's knight safely captures White's knight on c4, as no White piece or pawn can immediately recapture on the c4 square (the c2 pawn blocks the c1 rook, the d4 pawn cannot capture straight ahead, and the f1 bishop is blocked by the e2 pawn).
- White will most likely respond by playing `hxg4`, taking your attacked bishop on g4. 

Effectively, you are trading your hanging light-squared bishop for a free White knight, while saving your own knight that was under attack by the king. If you were to do anything else, for instance retreating the bishop (`g4h5`), White would just play `Kxd2` and you would lose your knight for absolutely nothing.

The best and most material-efficient move is to take the knight on c4 with your knight.

make_move d2c4

Player_Black (to Proxy):

thought
The board reveals that I am playing as Black and I am up a significant amount of material (including a Queen). However, my light-squared Bishop on g4 is currently under attack by White's h3 pawn. 

I must decide whether to save the Bishop or create a larger threat. 
One tempting option is the Knight check `c4e3+`. But let's look closely at how White could respond. If I play `c4e3+`, White's King can move to d2 (`Kd2`), moving out of check while simultaneously attacking my Knight on e3. Then I would find myself with two pieces hanging at the same time: my Knight on e3 (attacked by the King) and my Bishop on g4 (still attacked by the h3 pawn). This would result in an unnecessary loss of material.

Since I am already winning comfortably, the best and most principled approach is to simply save the attacked Bishop.
Between retreating to h5 or f5:
- `g4h5` keeps the Bishop on the kingside, but it remains undefended on that square.
- `g4f5` places the Bishop on a very active central diagonal. Furthermore, on f5, the Bishop is solidly defended by my own pawn on e6. It targets the queenside and controls important central squares without being exposed to any immediate attacks (White cannot easily play e2-e4 because of my d5 pawn).

Retreating the Bishop to the safely defended f5 square is structurally sound and preserves my massive material advantage without allowing any counterplay.

I will play `g4f5`.

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the current board position, you have a massive material advantage, but two of your pieces are currently hanging: your Knight on **e3** is attacked by the White King on **d2**, and your Bishop on **f5** is attacked by the White Bishop on **d3**. 

You can solve both problems brilliantly with a single forcing tactical move! 

By jumping your Knight to **c4**, you deliver a direct check to the White King. Since the White King cannot capture the Knight (as the **c4** square is defended by your pawn on **d5**), White is virtually forced to capture your Knight with their Bishop (**d3xc4**). You can then safely recapture with your pawn (**d5xc4**). 

This sequence successfully trades your hanging Knight for White's active Bishop, simplifying the position and completely removing the threat to your **f5** Bishop!

Action:
make_move e3c4

thought
The knight on e5 is currently under attack by the white pawns on d4 and f4. We must move this knight to a safe square. 

Looking at the available knight moves, moving it to d3 (`e5d3`) is a highly forcing and advantageous move. 
1. It captures the unprotected white pawn on d3.
2. It delivers a direct check to the white king on e1 (since a knight on d3 attacks e1).
3. It keeps the initiative, forcing the white king to move and giving us time to coordinate our other pieces without losing material.

This simple, forcing tactic wins material and improves our position significantly.
 
Let's proceed with this move. 
- 'make_move e5d3' perfectly executes this decision. 

Everything looks clear and safe. I will make this move now. 
_No other threats exist to prevent this execution._ 

(Board coordinates check: Black e5 Knight captures White d3 pawn, checking White e1 King)
- Knight on e5 moves to d3. e5 to d3 is a valid L-shape jump.
- d3 to e1 is also a valid L-shape, giving Check (+).
- White's King is on e1.
Perfect. I'm ready. 
_End of thought process_

make_move e5d3

Player_Black (to Proxy):

To find the best move, we need to evaluate the position of the White King.

Currently, the White King on f1 is completely trapped. Its flight squares are covered:
- **e1 and g1** are covered by your Bishop on f2.
- **e2 and g2** are covered by your Knight on f4.
- **f2** is occupied by your Bishop, which is currently defended by your other Knight on g4.

If you immediately try to deliver checkmate by moving your Knight from g4 to e3 (or h2), the g4 Knight will abandon its defense of the f2 Bishop. As a result, the White King would simply capture your Bishop (`Kxf2`), escaping the mating net.

We need a waiting move that assigns a new defender to the f2 Bishop, keeping the net closed so that our g4 Knight is free to jump and deliver checkmate.

Your Queen on d8 is perfectly positioned to take up this defense. By capturing the White pawn on h4 (`d8h4`), your Queen establishes a direct, unobstructed diagonal to f2 (h4 -> g3 -> f2). 

Here is why this is completely winning:
1. `Qxh4` defends the f2 Bishop.
2. The White King remains with exactly 0 legal moves.
3. White is not stalemated because their a2 pawn is free to push forward (a3 or a4). 
4. Once White wastes their turn pushing the a-pawn, you will follow up with `g4e3#`. The Knight will land on e3, delivering an unblockable check to the f1 King without obstructing your Queen's defense of f2, resulting in a beautiful checkmate!

make_move d8h4

Player_Black (to Proxy):

thought
The board reveals that our Black Queen is currently on d1, where it has infiltrated White's position. White's Rook on g1 is directly attacking our Queen along the first rank. We must address this threat to avoid losing our most valuable piece.

Looking at our Queen's legal moves, we have the option to capture White's Knight on e2 with `d1e2` (Qxe2). Let's evaluate if the e2 Knight is protected:
- White's King is on g2, which is too far to defend e2.
- White's Bishop on c1 is on a dark square, whereas e2 is a light square. 
- White's pawns are on c3, e3, and f2. Pawns capture diagonally forward, so neither f2 nor any other pawn defends e2.
- White's Rooks on a1 and g1 cannot see the e2 square.

Therefore, the Knight on e2 is completely undefended. By playing Qxe2, we not only move our Queen out of the path of White's g1 Rook but also win a full piece in the process.

Let's secure the advantage.
↑"make_move d1e2"

Player_Black (to Proxy):

Based on the current board position, White's king is exposed on g2, and you have a massive material advantage, including a very active Queen on e2. 

The move **e2e4** is extremely strong here. It captures White's completely undefended central pawn on e4 while simultaneously delivering a check to the White king on g2 along the open a8-h1 diagonal. 

Let's look at White's possible responses:
- If White tries to block the check by pushing the f2 pawn to f3 (`f3`), your Queen safely escapes (for example to c2, delivering another devastating check along the 2nd rank). 
- If White moves their King to f3 (`Kf3`), your Queen is safely defended by your Knight on f6, and the White King is drawn further into the center of the board where it is doomed. 
- If White moves the King to f1 or h3, you maintain a crushing initiative and material advantage.

There are no immediate tactical dangers, and taking the free central pawn with a tempo (check) is easily the best practical and principled choice.

make_move e2e4

Player_Black (to Proxy):

thought
The board shows that Black has a huge material advantage, and White's king is quite exposed on b1. 

Let's look for forcing moves, specifically checks. Black's Queen is on f3. The diagonal path from e4 to b1 is completely open (the d3 and c2 squares are empty), and there are no White pieces capable of blocking a check along this light-squared diagonal. The White Bishop on c1 is on a dark square, and the White pawns cannot move to block.

If we play `Qe4+` (f3e4), White's King will be forced to move. It cannot step onto c2 since that square is controlled by the Queen, meaning the King is strictly forced to move to a2. This pushes the White King further into the corner where it will be highly vulnerable to mating attacks involving your Knights (e.g., Nc6-d4 or Ne3-c4) and the Queen.

Let's execute this powerful check.
_make_move f3e4_

To secure a forced mate, Black can use the Queen and Knight to trap the White King. 

By moving the Queen to **b3**, you place the White King in check. The Queen is perfectly defended by the pawn on **a4**. Since the King cannot capture the Queen, and all other adjacent squares are occupied by White's own pawns or covered by your Queen, White's King is forced into the corner on **a1**.
